\noindent\begin{tikzpicture}[remember picture,overlay,x=1bp,y=1bp,shift={(current page.north west)}]
  \fill[ALIUSC767171] (76.253bp,-65.268bp) rectangle ++(443.378bp,-0.233bp);
  \fill[ALIUSC767171] (76.253bp,-777.332bp) rectangle ++(443.378bp,-0.466bp);
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{46.516}{344.963}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{Michael Winkelman - The evolutionary neuroanthropology of consciousness}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{505.773}{46.516}{14.471}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{69}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{81.223}{408.728}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{2015) and the reduction in oscillatory activity and power in posterior and}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{97.550}{408.709}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{frontal association cortices (Muthukumaraswamy et al., 2013). This involves a}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{114.109}{408.640}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{decrease in the functional coupling of the frontal cortex with the medial}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{130.669}{408.728}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{temporal lobe, as well as of the medial prefrontal cortex with the posterior}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{146.995}{408.741}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{cingulate cortex. Consequently, the lower brain dynamics involving}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{163.555}{408.683}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{ascending circuitry are released, providing a strong input to the frontal cortex}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{180.115}{408.721}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{from the ancient brain systems. This dynamic is hypothesized as the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{196.441}{408.633}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{mechanism that releases the innate modules and promotes their}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{213.001}{325.701}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{manifestations in consciousness (also see Winkelman, 2017b).}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{241.222}{408.707}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{A principal effect of psychedelics involves production of hypersynchronous}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{257.548}{405.511}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{ascending slow wave brain discharges in the hippocampal-septal-reticular-}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{274.108}{408.739}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{raphe circuit that impose impulses from the ancient lower stratum of the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{290.668}{408.707}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{brain on the frontal areas (Mandell, 1980). This pattern of psychedelic action}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{306.994}{408.825}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{on the brain is shared by many other agents and conditions that alter}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{323.554}{408.687}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{consciousness (Winkelman, 2011). Alterations of consciousness produced by}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{339.880}{408.675}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{behavioral and physiological conditions, as well as pharmacological agents,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{356.440}{408.637}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{causes a reduction in the serotonin inhibition to the hippocampal cells, which}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{372.999}{408.707}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{results in an increase in slow-wave EEG activity in the hippocampal-septal}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{389.559}{42.974}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{region.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{417.547}{408.650}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{Research on the mechanisms of action of psychedelics on the major cortical}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{434.107}{408.702}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{loops (Vollenweider \& Geyer, 2001) illustrates these effects. Psychedelic}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{450.433}{408.711}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{effects on the cortico-striato-thalamo-cortical (CSTC) feedback loops and}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{466.993}{408.748}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{their regulatory effects on the gating systems of lower levels of the brain alter}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{483.552}{408.681}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{consciousness. This psychedelic interruption of the CSTC loops and their}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{499.879}{408.651}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{inhibitory function release the lower brain structures' sensory gating and}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{516.438}{408.827}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{enhance the flow of information to the frontal areas of the brain. Psychedelics}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{532.998}{408.733}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{interruption of thalamic screening results in a flood of information from}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{549.558}{173.011}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{these ancient levels of the brain.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{577.546}{408.699}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{Alonso et al. (2015) confirmed this psychedelic enhancement of a bottom-up}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{593.872}{408.628}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{information dynamic by psychedelics with ayahuasca. They assessed}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{610.432}{408.712}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{ayahuasca-induced changes in directionality of information flow in the brain,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{626.992}{408.691}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{with changes in the connectivity of brain oscillations. These changes involved}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{643.318}{408.682}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{a disruption of the normal coupling between anterior and posterior areas of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{659.878}{408.806}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{the brain that resulted from reduction in the influence of frontal brain areas}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{676.437}{408.686}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{over the posterior areas. This reduction was accompanied by increases in the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{692.764}{408.647}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{influence of posterior brain regions on the frontal anterior areas. "These}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{709.323}{408.767}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{results suggest that psychedelics induce a temporary disruption of neural}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{725.883}{408.699}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{hierarchies by reducing top-down control and increasing bottom-up}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{742.443}{351.727}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{information transfer in the human brain" (Alonso et al. 2015, p. 1).}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{783.303}{117.883}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{ALIUS Bulletin n°3 (2019)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{402.025}{783.303}{116.144}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{aliusresearch.org/bulletin}
\end{tikzpicture}

\noindent\begin{tikzpicture}[remember picture,overlay,x=1bp,y=1bp,shift={(current page.north west)}]
  \fill[ALIUSC767171] (76.253bp,-65.268bp) rectangle ++(443.378bp,-0.233bp);
  \fill[ALIUSC767171] (76.253bp,-777.332bp) rectangle ++(443.378bp,-0.466bp);
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{46.516}{344.963}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{Michael Winkelman - The evolutionary neuroanthropology of consciousness}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{505.773}{46.516}{14.471}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{70}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{81.223}{408.742}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{The release of these lower brain areas is the likely cause of the enhanced}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{97.550}{408.625}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{operation of the innate modular operators of the brain. These operators}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{114.109}{408.701}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{reflect unconscious cognitive processes that were acquired deep in evolution,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{130.669}{408.735}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{with some apparently widely shared by other primates and mammals. These}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{146.995}{408.636}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{ancient roots of these operators suggest that they are associated with the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{163.555}{408.698}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{function of our ancient brain structures. Winkelman (2010a) has detailed how}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{180.115}{408.675}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{many features of shamanic alterations of consciousness can be explained by}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{196.441}{408.652}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{reference to the operation of these ancient brain structures, particularly the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{213.001}{128.468}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{paleomammalian brain.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{241.051}{442.869}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Regardless of the question of knowing which electrophysiological rhythm underlies the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{255.745}{442.864}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{integrative mode of consciousness one might be tempted to challenge the view that}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{270.206}{129.125}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{such a mode is indeed}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{210.612}{270.206}{51.484}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{integrative}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{262.092}{270.206}{258.447}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{. In your initial definition, what you meant by}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{284.899}{442.867}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{integration was that different parts of the brain that are usually disconnected suddenly}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{299.360}{442.705}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{become connected. An obvious way to measure such (dis)connectivity in the brain is to}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{314.054}{442.882}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{look at functional connectivity (Friston, 2011). Fortunately, in recent years, several}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{328.514}{442.863}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{studies have looked at this dimension of the psychedelic experience. By and large, the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{343.208}{442.872}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{pattern of finding is that functional connectivity is locally decreased but globally}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{357.668}{442.881}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{increased, a point that you duly acknowledge and nicely discuss in your recent work}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{372.362}{442.823}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{(Winkelman, 2017b, pp. 7–8). For example, it has been shown that within the default}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{386.823}{442.854}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{mode network, connectivity is weakened, but conversely different areas of this}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{401.516}{442.863}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{network start talking to other areas and networks from which they are usually}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{415.977}{442.864}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{disconnected (Carhart-Harris et al., 2014). Moreover, in some cases, the large-scale}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{430.671}{442.836}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{connectivity between networks can even be decreased: e.g., with psilocybin, the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{445.131}{442.832}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{connectivity between visual and sensorimotor areas is weakened (Roseman, Leech,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{459.825}{318.892}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Feilding, Nutt, \& Carhart-Harris, 2014). The key lesson is that}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{396.814}{459.825}{32.380}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{overall}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{431.817}{459.825}{88.719}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{psychointegrators}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{474.519}{140.305}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{do not maximize connectivity}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{217.960}{474.519}{4.740}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{500.641}{442.852}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{At this point of the discussion, it will be useful to introduce two concepts: complexity}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{515.102}{442.837}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{and neural criticality. The first concept was developed by Giulio Tononi (Tononi,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{529.795}{442.906}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Edelman, \& Sporns, 1998; Tononi, Sporns, \& Edelman, 1996). It is a measure combining}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{544.256}{443.066}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{two indexes: segregation and integration. If areas of the brain are excessively}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{558.950}{442.941}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{segregated, complexity will be low and this will result in a lack of conscious experience}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{573.410}{355.929}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{(Boly et al., 2012; Tagliazucchi et al., 2013) (see bottom right circle in}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{433.789}{573.410}{39.824}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{Figure 2}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{473.629}{573.410}{46.869}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{). On the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{588.104}{442.924}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{other hand, if areas of the brain turn out to be excessively integrated, complexity will}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{602.564}{442.862}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{also be too low for consciousness to emerge (Arthuis et al., 2009; Blumenfeld, 2012)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{617.258}{132.500}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{(see bottom left circle in}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{211.887}{617.258}{41.292}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{Figure 2}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{253.203}{617.258}{267.305}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{). Complexity is the highest when segregation and}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{631.719}{442.843}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{integration are optimally balanced. This is typically the case in the everyday waking}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{646.413}{30.741}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{state.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{783.303}{117.883}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{ALIUS Bulletin n°3 (2019)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{402.025}{783.303}{116.144}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{aliusresearch.org/bulletin}
\end{tikzpicture}

\noindent\begin{tikzpicture}[remember picture,overlay,x=1bp,y=1bp,shift={(current page.north west)}]
  \fill[ALIUSC767171] (76.253bp,-65.268bp) rectangle ++(443.378bp,-0.233bp);
  \fill[ALIUSC767171] (76.253bp,-777.332bp) rectangle ++(443.378bp,-0.466bp);
  \node[anchor=north west,inner sep=0pt,outer sep=0pt] at (173.978bp,-94.704bp) {\includegraphics[width=250.113bp,height=184.012bp]{Interviews/Issue03/Winkelman_Fortier/assets/alius-extracted-image-p027-b009.png}};
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{46.516}{344.963}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{Michael Winkelman - The evolutionary neuroanthropology of consciousness}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{505.773}{46.516}{14.471}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{71}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{141.557}{301.925}{315.052}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{Figure 2. Three combinations of brain segregation and integration}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{216.403}{316.619}{165.359}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{(from: Tononi et al., 1998, p. 478)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{356.036}{442.806}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Another concept, criticality, has many points in common with that of complexity}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{370.730}{442.857}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{(Tagliazucchi \& Chialvo, 2013; Timme et al., 2016). It refers to power-law distributed}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{385.190}{442.869}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{physical phenomena that often take place in phase transition. The study of neuronal}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{399.884}{442.859}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{avalanches in the brain has also revealed the presence of such power-law distributed}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{414.344}{442.862}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{firing of neurons (Beggs \& Plenz, 2003; Chialvo, Balenzuela, \& Fraiman, 2008).}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{429.038}{442.835}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Interestingly, critical states largely overlap with complex states; furthermore, Tononi's}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{443.499}{442.858}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{excessively segregated states share many properties of sub-critical states and}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{458.192}{281.604}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{excessively integrated states with super-critical states.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{484.315}{443.016}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Now, recent research conducted mainly at Imperial College has demonstrated that}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{499.008}{442.899}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{psychedelic states are very close to criticality—in fact, even closer than normal waking}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{513.469}{442.868}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{states (Atasoy et al., 2017; Carhart-Harris, 2018; Carhart-Harris et al., 2014). This is}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{528.163}{442.862}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{consistent with the aforementioned findings to the effect that serotonergic}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{542.623}{217.213}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{hallucinogens do not increase connectivity}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{294.298}{542.623}{49.977}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{simpliciter}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{344.290}{542.623}{176.206}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{but rather do so in a very balanced}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{557.317}{442.825}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{way (connectivity is rearranged through both increase and decrease of connectivity}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{571.778}{442.895}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{patterns). It appears, then, that psychedelics are not properly speaking}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{586.471}{36.306}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{psycho}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{113.961}{586.471}{52.576}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{integrators}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{166.529}{586.471}{353.954}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{. A true psychointegrator would be a compound able to trigger states}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{600.932}{442.882}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{with the highest integration and the lowest segregation possible (bottom left circle in}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{615.626}{40.503}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{Figure 2}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{118.174}{615.626}{402.350}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{). It seems that this is not what serotonergic hallucinogens do; instead, this is}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{630.086}{442.863}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{what epileptic seizures do (Arthuis et al., 2009; Meisel, Storch, Hallmeyer-Elgner,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{644.780}{442.862}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Bullmore, \& Gross, 2012; Priesemann, Valderrama, Wibral, \& Le Van Quyen, 2013).}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{659.240}{442.884}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Therefore, don't you think that only epileptic seizures should deserve the title of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{673.934}{4.039}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{"}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{81.691}{673.934}{53.824}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{integrative}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{137.347}{673.934}{158.171}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{mode of consciousness" (i.e., "}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{295.518}{673.934}{59.285}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{supercritical}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{354.797}{673.934}{165.721}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{mode of consciousness")? And}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{688.395}{442.857}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{don't you think that serotonergic hallucinogens should rather be called}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{703.088}{442.870}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{"psychocomplexifiers" or "psychocriticalizers" and the mode of consciousness they}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{717.782}{60.233}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{induce the "}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{137.885}{717.782}{42.454}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{complex}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{180.352}{717.782}{13.739}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{or}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{194.091}{717.782}{32.739}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{critical}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{226.828}{717.782}{131.372}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{mode of consciousness?"}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{783.303}{117.883}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{ALIUS Bulletin n°3 (2019)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{402.025}{783.303}{116.144}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{aliusresearch.org/bulletin}

  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{224.662}{443.703}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{increased connectivity of areas and networks from which they are usually}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{241.222}{443.681}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{disconnected. So I would say this new generation of research is supporting the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{257.548}{443.678}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{notion of some forms of enhanced connectivity, albeit at the expense of breakdown}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{274.108}{443.686}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{of the normal connectivity networks of the default mode network and prefrontal}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{290.668}{42.348}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{cortex.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{318.718}{442.844}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{There is arguably another reason for thinking that psychedelics are not genuine}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{333.412}{442.849}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{psychointegrators. According to you, the integrative mode of consciousness coincides}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{347.873}{254.419}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{with a "loss of hippocampal CA3 modulation}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{336.649}{347.873}{183.869}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{removes regulatory input from the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{362.566}{229.247}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{environment, leaving the 'inside world' dominant}

  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{377.027}{442.880}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{In another passage you also argue that "[t]he highly integrated internal visionary states}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{391.721}{326.538}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{of the shaman involve a reduction of other inputs, in essence}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{405.726}{391.721}{114.788}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{a reduction of external}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{406.181}{96.368}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{sensory information}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{174.042}{406.181}{346.477}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{and bodily input" (Winkelman, 2010a, p. 32, my emphasis). In sum,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{420.875}{442.945}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{in the integrative mode of consciousness, the central nervous system becomes less}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{435.335}{442.864}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{sensitive to the "external world" and more sensitive to the "internal world" (i.e., to itself).}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{450.029}{442.862}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{If so, then, it does not seem that serotonergic hallucinogens are psychointegrators,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{464.490}{262.898}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{because like any (near) critical state, they induce an}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{340.455}{464.490}{48.344}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{increased}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{388.718}{464.490}{131.747}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{sensitivity to stimuli from}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{479.184}{442.890}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{the external world (Atasoy et al., 2017; Carhart-Harris, 2018). As Enzo Tagliazucchi}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{493.644}{442.794}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{puts it, "physical systems at criticality present a maximal susceptibility, i.e. a maximal}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{508.338}{442.891}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{response to external perturbations" (2017, p. 145). In other words, it appears that}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{522.798}{442.862}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{psychedelics do the contrary to what a psychointegrator is purported to do: they}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{537.492}{442.725}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{increase—rather than decrease—responses to the external world. Don't you think this}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{551.953}{442.990}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{supports the view that psychointegrators (a.k.a. psychedelics) would be better}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{566.646}{171.488}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{described as psychocriticalizers?}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{592.706}{443.694}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{Since the earliest studies of the psychedelics in the context of psychiatry it was}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{609.266}{443.680}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{recognized that the primary determinants of the effects are from set and setting, the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{625.592}{443.691}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{individual's expectations and the influences in the environment. So there is a lot of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{642.152}{443.729}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{variation in terms of what psychedelics can produce. Some cultures used ayahuasca}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{658.711}{443.677}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{as preparation for headhunting and warfare, dancing for hours before leaving during}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{675.038}{443.835}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{the night to carry out raids. Other cultures use ayahuasca for journeying across the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{691.597}{443.702}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{cosmos or healing. The same substance can produce diverse outcomes, depending}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{708.157}{443.707}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{on how you engage with it. There is also some confusion promoted in recent brain}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{724.483}{443.709}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{research from a failure to pay attention to the mode of administration of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{783.303}{117.883}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{ALIUS Bulletin n°3 (2019)}

  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{46.516}{344.963}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{Michael Winkelman - The evolutionary neuroanthropology of consciousness}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{505.773}{46.516}{14.471}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{73}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{81.223}{443.566}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{psilocybin—whether it is drunk or injected. It makes a big difference whether you}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{97.783}{407.685}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{chew coca leaves or inject cocaine, we should expect the same for psilocybin.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{125.834}{442.927}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{You have endorsed a strong perennialist approach in your work on the contents of the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{140.294}{442.900}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{integrative mode of consciousness. For example, according to you, mystical and}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{154.988}{442.858}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{psychedelic states are very similar because they both tap into the same evolutionary}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{169.448}{442.858}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{mechanisms: "The relationships among natural and drug-induced alterations of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{184.142}{442.852}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{consciousness must be understood from an evolutionary perspective. This reveals}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{198.603}{442.891}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{altered consciousness to be related to endogenous mechanisms that are triggered by}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{213.296}{442.869}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{both ancient evolutionary adaptations and more recently acquired propensities to use}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{227.757}{442.862}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{exogenous sources of substances to alter consciousness" (Winkelman, 2010a, p. 27).}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{242.451}{442.866}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{All integrative states seem to share common themes, images, and features. As you note,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{256.911}{442.965}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{"[t]he apparent similarities in psychedelic entities and various other types of entity}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{271.605}{442.870}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{experiences found across cultures, time, and diverse conditions for altering}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{286.065}{442.916}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{consciousness suggest that an explanation be sought within innate functions of the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{300.759}{442.878}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{human brain" (Winkelman, 2018a, p. 3). Such a view echoes the Jungian theory of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{315.220}{442.799}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{archetypes according to which the cognitive and perceptual features that are}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{329.914}{442.906}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{universally found across cultures are to be explained in terms of underlying}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{344.374}{442.862}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{unconscious archetypes (Laughlin, 2011, Chapter 10; Laughlin et al., 1990, p. 134;}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{359.068}{442.852}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Winkelman, 2010a, p. 220, 2018a, p. 7; Winkelman \& Baker, 2010, pp. 194–198). Your}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{373.528}{442.808}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{perennialist evolutionary approach raises two questions: (1) why should universal}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{388.222}{442.868}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{cognitive features be necessarily explained in evolutionary terms?; and, (2) what is the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{402.916}{209.254}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{actual evidence in favor of perennialism?}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{429.038}{442.824}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Let me start by examining the first question. Is it true that universality is a cue to}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{443.499}{442.909}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{innateness? Well, if it is, it could be argued that it is a very inaccurate cue. There are}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{458.192}{442.870}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{plenty of cognitive and perceptual phenomena that are universal and probably not}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{472.653}{442.891}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{innate. For example, all humans have the prior expectation encoded in their visual}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{487.347}{442.882}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{system that light is coming from above (Sun \& Perona, 1998). Among other things, the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{501.807}{442.940}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{famous concave/convex circle illusion is explained by this prior. Given the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{516.501}{442.870}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{pervasiveness of such a prior one may expect it to be innate. But it seems that it is not}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{530.961}{442.854}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{since it can be changed by experience (Adams, Graf, \& Ernst, 2004). The universality of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{545.655}{442.997}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{the light-from-above prior appears to be learned. However, given that the structure of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{560.116}{440.521}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{the environment is everywhere the same (i.e., light always comes from above in non-}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{574.810}{442.995}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{artificial environments), in this case, learning (absence of innateness) coincides with}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{589.270}{442.864}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{universality. As students of natural scene statistics have amply demonstrated, many}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{603.964}{442.961}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{universal features of the perceptual system and of perceptual experience can be}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{618.424}{442.839}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{explained by non-innate learning mechanisms combined with stable environmental}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{633.118}{268.201}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{structures (Geisler \& Kersten, 2002; Geisler, 2008).}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{659.240}{442.837}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Another problem with the view that universality can be used as a cue to innateness is}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{673.934}{442.831}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{that it overlooks the possibility of diffusion phenomena. For instance, many apparently}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{688.395}{442.839}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{universal features of myths can be thoroughly explained by cultural diffusion. To take a}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{703.088}{442.829}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{specific example, some authors have proposed evolutionary accounts of the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{717.549}{442.812}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{widespread dragon motif—both in iconography and myths and legends (Jones, 2016;}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{732.243}{442.894}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Sperber, 1996b; Wengrow, 2013). If this motif is so pervasive, it is argued, it is because}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{746.703}{442.903}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{our brain is structured in such a way that it easily triggers and encodes chimeric}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{783.303}{117.883}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{ALIUS Bulletin n°3 (2019)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{402.025}{783.303}{116.144}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{aliusresearch.org/bulletin}
\end{tikzpicture}

  \fill[ALIUSCFFFFFF] (77.652bp,-311.564bp) rectangle ++(440.580bp,-14.460bp);
  \fill[ALIUSCFFFFFF] (77.652bp,-326.024bp) rectangle ++(440.580bp,-14.694bp);
  \fill[ALIUSCFFFFFF] (77.652bp,-340.718bp) rectangle ++(440.580bp,-14.461bp);
  \fill[ALIUSCFFFFFF] (77.652bp,-355.178bp) rectangle ++(440.580bp,-14.694bp);
  \fill[ALIUSCFFFFFF] (77.652bp,-369.872bp) rectangle ++(344.953bp,-14.694bp);
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{46.516}{344.963}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{Michael Winkelman - The evolutionary neuroanthropology of consciousness}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{505.773}{46.516}{14.471}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{74}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{81.286}{268.303}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{creatures. But such an explanation sounds way too}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{347.074}{81.286}{33.049}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{ad hoc}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{380.133}{81.286}{140.421}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{. As a matter of fact, it has}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{95.746}{442.793}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{been shown that the distribution of the mythological dragon motif can be accounted for}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{110.440}{442.883}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{merely in diffusionist terms (d'Huy, 2013, 2014, 2016). The phylogeny of cultural}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{124.901}{442.832}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{representations reveals how the dragon motif has been gradually modified and updated}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{139.594}{442.823}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{from its earliest versions (in African cultures) to its latest versions (in Amerindian}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{154.055}{442.838}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{cultures). More generally, as demonstrated by population genetics, the phylogeny of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{168.749}{442.860}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{myths seems to largely overlap with prehistoric human migrations (Korotayev \&}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{183.209}{442.847}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Khaltourina, 2011). This overlap provides strong evidence in favor of the pervasiveness}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{197.903}{442.897}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{of diffusion phenomena. As a result, many universal traits of human culture can be}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{212.363}{442.790}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{explained by resorting to the prehistory of migrations and borrowings rather than by}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{227.057}{192.345}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{positing cognitive adaptive functions.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{253.179}{442.851}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{In sum, it seems that the burden of proof is on the proponent of psycho-evolutionary}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{267.873}{442.870}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{and nativist explanations. For example, when you write that "[t]he widespread}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{282.334}{442.890}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{manifestation of these visual elements under diverse circumstances attests to the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{297.028}{442.874}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{elicitation of these innate mechanisms in producing these characteristic psychedelic}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{311.488}{442.868}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{experiences" (Winkelman, 2017b, p. 9), it could be objected that pinpointing the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{326.182}{442.853}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{universal distribution of a trait is not enough to demonstrate its evolutionary origin. In}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{340.642}{442.666}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{fact, some evolutionary psychologists have even called for a thorough dissociation}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{355.336}{442.844}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{between being an innate adaptive function and being universal (e.g., Apicella \& Barrett,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{370.030}{347.255}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{2016; C. Barrett \& Kurzban, 2006). What is your view on this issue?}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{396.090}{443.636}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{"Why should universal cognitive features be necessarily explained in evolutionary}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{412.416}{443.825}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{terms?" Well if you really have a more compelling line of explanation besides the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{428.976}{443.621}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{innate tendencies and their adaptive potentials, then offer it. I think the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{445.535}{443.651}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{combination of the cross-cultural manifestations in religion and their various}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{461.862}{443.655}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{putatively adaptive functions is the most compelling argument. What would you}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{478.421}{49.817}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{propose?}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{506.705}{442.957}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Let me now turn to the second question: what is the actual evidence for perennialism?}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{521.166}{442.980}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{First, it should be said that Jung, one of the main inspirations of neuroanthropological}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{535.859}{442.816}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{perennialism, based his theory on a somewhat biased ethnographic database: he}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{550.320}{440.521}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{misinterpreted several ethnographic cases and carefully ignored numerous counter-}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{565.014}{442.859}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{examples which refute the theory of archetypes (Le Quellec, 2013, Chapters 8–9). But}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{579.474}{442.759}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{rather than focusing on the details of Jung's theory it is more interesting to examine}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{594.168}{442.862}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{what the evidence for perennialism is in the specific case of hallucinogenic experiences.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{620.290}{442.853}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{According to you, hallucinogens offer a pharmacological model of mysticism whose}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{634.984}{289.640}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{relevance goes far beyond drug-induced mystical states:}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{661.106}{407.910}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Similarities in psychedelic-induced visionary experiences and those produced by}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{675.800}{407.865}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{practices such as meditation and hypnosis and pathological conditions such as}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{690.261}{407.941}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{epilepsy indicate the need for a general model explaining visionary experiences.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{704.954}{130.880}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{(Winkelman, 2017b, p. 1)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{731.077}{407.882}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{The fundamental similarities of psychedelic-induced and naturally-induced}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{745.770}{407.834}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{mystical experiences (Smith, 2000; Yaden et al., 2017) support the classic}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{783.303}{117.883}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{ALIUS Bulletin n°3 (2019)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{402.025}{783.303}{116.144}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{aliusresearch.org/bulletin}
\end{tikzpicture}

  \fill[ALIUSC767171] (76.253bp,-65.268bp) rectangle ++(443.378bp,-0.233bp);
  \fill[ALIUSC767171] (76.253bp,-777.332bp) rectangle ++(443.378bp,-0.466bp);
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{46.516}{344.963}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{Michael Winkelman - The evolutionary neuroanthropology of consciousness}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{505.773}{46.516}{14.471}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{75}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{81.286}{407.875}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{perennialist view of fundamental commonalities to mystical experiences across}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{95.746}{407.819}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{cultures and their independence of the mode of induction. (Winkelman, 2017b,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{110.440}{24.273}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{p. 5)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{136.562}{442.864}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{It seems that the conflation of hallucinogenic states and other states like epileptic}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{151.256}{442.972}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{seizures and meditation could be resisted on several grounds. First, as we have seen}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{165.717}{442.806}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{before, from a brain-system perspective, epileptic seizures are very different from}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{180.410}{442.856}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{serotonergic-induced states: the former are supercritical whereas the latter are (near)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{194.871}{442.863}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{critical. By the same token, although meditation and serotonergic-induced states have}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{209.565}{442.790}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{certainly many things in common, they nevertheless exhibit striking neurophysiological}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{224.025}{442.862}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{differences (Millière, Carhart-Harris, Roseman, Trautwein, \& Berkovich-Ohana, 2018).}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{238.719}{442.861}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Even without looking at the differences between hallucinogenic and non-hallucinogenic}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{253.179}{443.024}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{altered states and focusing only on hallucinogenic experiences, perennialism does not}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{267.873}{442.848}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{seem very compelling. Namely, when looking at the diversity of hallucinogenic}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{282.334}{442.832}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{experiences, it is difficult to see what kind of phenomenological feature truly fit with the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{297.028}{120.663}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{theory of perennialism.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{323.150}{442.863}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Before further elaborating on this anti-perennialist argument, it will be helpful to clarify}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{337.844}{442.879}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{what is meant here by "hallucinogens." In your article on psychointegrators you put}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{352.537}{167.807}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{forward the following definition:}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{378.660}{407.771}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Classification as a hallucinogen (or psychedelic) has not been based upon}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{393.120}{407.860}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{specific chemistry or physiology, but upon effects on human experience,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{407.814}{372.915}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{producing visions, voices and effects upon perception, mood, and thought}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{485.126}{407.814}{33.041}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{in non-}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{422.274}{52.394}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLightItalic}{12.128}{toxic doses}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{165.046}{422.274}{355.315}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{(Siegel, 1984). This distinguishes them from substances that produce}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{436.968}{383.067}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{hallucinations because of toxicity. (Winkelman, 2001, p. 220, my emphasis)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{463.090}{442.871}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Thus, according to this definition, hallucinogens and psychedelics are synonymous. This}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{477.784}{442.869}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{is no surprise given that you cite serotonergic hallucinogens (e.g., LSD, mescaline,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{492.245}{442.947}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{psilocybin) as prototypical examples of hallucinogens (a.k.a. psychedelics). What is less}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{506.938}{442.844}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{clear is why you suggest hallucinogens should be restricted to compounds whose}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{521.399}{442.832}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{"mechanisms of action involv[e] intervention in serotonin pathways" (Winkelman,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{536.093}{442.908}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{2001, p. 220). Why not also include other classes of hallucinogens whose mechanisms}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{550.553}{442.941}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{are not serotonergic? Following the above definition of hallucinogens, it seems that}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{565.014}{393.489}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{other neuropharmacological classes should also be included: notably,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{482.089}{565.014}{38.427}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{-opioid}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{476.733}{565.438}{5.349}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontCalibri}{12.128}{κ}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{580.407}{442.846}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{hallucinogens (e.g., salvia divinorum), antiglutamatergic hallucinogens (e.g., ketamine),}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{595.101}{442.833}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{antihistaminergic hallucinogens (e.g., diphenhydramine), antimuscarinic hallucinogens}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{609.561}{442.884}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{(e.g., scopolamine), and hybrid hallucinogens (e.g., ibogaine). As we can see, there are}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{624.255}{442.737}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{various classes of hallucinogens and all of them fulfill the definitional criteria that you}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{638.716}{442.897}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{put forward in the above passage—including, it must be stressed, antimuscarinic}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{653.409}{442.862}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{hallucinogens, which can indeed be toxic at high doses (Winkelman, 2001, p. 232, f.n. 1),}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{667.870}{442.840}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{but which remain quite safe at hallucinogenic doses: as a matter of fact, the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{682.564}{442.765}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{hallucinogenic threshold of these compounds is much lower than the toxicity threshold}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{697.024}{442.844}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{(Gyermek, 1998, pp. 352–353). For example, in the experiments at Edgewood Arsenal,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{711.718}{442.809}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{the highest doses of BZ (3-Quinuclidinyl benzilate) given to participants were never}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{726.179}{133.302}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{higher than 10\% of the LD}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{219.987}{726.179}{300.594}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{(the median lethal dose). Most of the time, doses that were}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{210.969}{731.485}{8.973}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{7.697}{50}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{740.872}{209.710}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{administered did not exceed 3\% of the LD}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{296.395}{740.872}{224.219}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{and yet copious hallucinations were already}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{287.377}{746.179}{8.973}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{7.697}{50}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{783.303}{117.883}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{ALIUS Bulletin n°3 (2019)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{402.025}{783.303}{116.144}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{aliusresearch.org/bulletin}

\noindent\begin{tikzpicture}[remember picture,overlay,x=1bp,y=1bp,shift={(current page.north west)}]
  \fill[ALIUSC767171] (76.253bp,-65.268bp) rectangle ++(443.378bp,-0.233bp);
  \fill[ALIUSC767171] (76.253bp,-777.332bp) rectangle ++(443.378bp,-0.466bp);
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{46.516}{344.963}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{Michael Winkelman - The evolutionary neuroanthropology of consciousness}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{505.773}{46.516}{14.471}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{76}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{81.286}{442.862}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{observed at such doses (Panel on Anticholinesterase Chemicals, Panel on}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{95.746}{442.733}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Anticholinergic Chemicals, Committee on Toxicology, Board on Toxicology and}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{110.440}{376.719}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Environmental Health Hazards, \& Assembly of Life Sciences, 1982, p. 61).}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{136.562}{442.972}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Now, the objection that could be raised against any perennialist account of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{151.256}{442.837}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{hallucinogens would go like this: you argue that the phenomenology of hallucinogens}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{165.717}{440.521}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{support perennialism (the view that all hallucinogenic experiences—and other non-}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{180.410}{442.821}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{chemically-induced altered states—share a common phenomenological core), but this}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{194.871}{442.873}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{is because you take only serotonergic hallucinogens into account; if you were to include}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{209.565}{442.832}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{in your analysis other classes of hallucinogens, you would then realize that massive}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{224.025}{442.876}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{differences exist between these compounds, and as a result, you would acknowledge}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{238.719}{198.929}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{that perennialism does not hold water.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{264.841}{442.903}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Let me flesh out this anti-perennialist objection with three concrete examples. It is very}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{279.535}{442.866}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{common for people to find themselves transformed into artefacts under the effect of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{293.995}{442.898}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{salvia divinorum (they are typically metamorphosed into wheels or pieces of furniture}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{308.689}{442.875}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{surrounding them); by contrast, this is never reported in serotonergic-induced trips}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{323.150}{442.860}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{(Fortier, In preparation, Chapter 4). Similarly, antimuscarinics strikingly differ from}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{337.844}{442.873}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{serotonergics in their effects. While the latter often induce extraordinary}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{352.304}{442.847}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{hallucinations (spirits, anthropomorphic entities, chimeras, etc.) the former only induce}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{366.998}{442.875}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{hallucinations of very ordinary and mundane objects (humans, animals, everyday}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{381.458}{442.866}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{artefacts, etc.) (Fortier, In preparation, Chapter 4, 2018a, 2018b, 2019; Ketchum,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{396.152}{442.774}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{2006; Ketchum et al., 1973). Even entoptic hallucinations (Billock \& Tsou, 2012; Klüver,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{410.613}{442.857}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{1966; Lewis-Williams \& Dowson, 1988), which are often taken to be a universal feature}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{425.306}{442.873}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{of hallucinogenic experiences, are completely absent from antihistaminergic- and}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{440.000}{380.297}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{antimuscarinic-induced hallucinations (Fortier, In preparation, Chapter 4).}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{466.122}{442.691}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{After having examined the outstanding diversity of features induced by each class of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{480.583}{442.862}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{hallucinogens, it is very difficult to see what kind of phenomenological feature could be}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{495.277}{442.857}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{argued to constitute the common perennial core of these experiences. Importantly, if}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{509.737}{443.100}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{this criticism of perennialism is correct, then hallucinogens (as defined above) cannot}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{524.431}{442.868}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{be said to form a single mode of consciousness anymore. Moreover, the contents of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{538.891}{442.882}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{these hallucinogenic experiences cannot be said to share a common core with other}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{553.585}{442.875}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{nonchemically-induced mystical states because what is meant by "hallucinogenic}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{568.279}{442.862}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{experience" covers very heterogeneous experiences with no identifiable common core.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{594.401}{442.836}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Is this line of criticism of perennialism sound to you? If so, do you think that the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{608.862}{442.844}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{evolutionary perennialist account of hallucinogenic experiences can nonetheless be}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{623.556}{237.904}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{salvaged from this anti-perennialist objection?}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{649.615}{443.665}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{"What is the actual evidence in favor of perennialism?" The universal features of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{666.175}{443.663}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{religious and spiritual belief and practice, the cross-cultural variation in such}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{682.734}{443.652}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{patterns as a function of social complexity, the cross-cultural features of shamans,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{699.061}{443.742}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{the universal manifestations of shamanistic healers, the fundamental similarity in}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{715.621}{443.660}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{many forms of meditative experience across diverse and disparate traditions, the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{783.303}{117.883}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{ALIUS Bulletin n°3 (2019)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{402.025}{783.303}{116.144}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{aliusresearch.org/bulletin}
\end{tikzpicture}

  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{219.764}{408.722}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{2010, 2013, 2016). This is not to say that psychedelics only share common}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{236.324}{408.696}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{effects, they also have distinctive effects, neurologically as well as}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{252.884}{408.679}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{phenomenologically (see: Ray, 2010, 2016). In assessing the different profiles}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{269.210}{408.690}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{of neurotransmitter interactions by various types of psychedelics, Ray (2016,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{285.770}{408.638}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{p. 49) noted that "[m]ost of the drugs studied interact with multiple receptors,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{302.329}{408.628}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{and most of the receptors studied interact with multiple drugs"  This leads}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{318.656}{408.743}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{Ray (2010, 2012) to challenge the dominant theory of psychedelic action as}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{335.215}{259.667}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{being primarily mediated by effects at the 5-HT}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{375.674}{335.215}{145.652}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{receptors. Examination of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{372.353}{341.105}{3.282}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{8.163}{2}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{351.542}{408.752}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{the relative affinity of various psychedelics for a wide range of receptors (Ray}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{368.101}{408.693}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{2010, p. 22 and 41) found that "LSD has the strongest interaction collectively}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{384.661}{408.716}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{with the five dopamine receptors […] [and] DMT has the strongest interaction}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{401.221}{189.786}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{with any single dopamine receptor.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{429.209}{408.686}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{Rolland and colleagues (2014) noted that hallucinations—or perhaps less}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{445.768}{408.702}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{pejoratively "visionary experiences"—may be induced by a variety}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{462.095}{408.705}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{pharmacological mechanisms, including the hyperactivation of dopamine}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{478.655}{408.677}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{receptors, such as that caused by psychostimulants; stimulation of serotonin}

  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{146.330}{495.214}{375.053}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{receptors targeted by psychedelics; and the blockage of glutamate}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{137.194}{501.104}{9.068}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{8.163}{2A}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{511.541}{408.653}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{NMDA receptors caused by dissociative anesthetics. They proposed that}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{528.100}{30.282}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{these}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{156.093}{528.100}{49.891}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{different}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{219.159}{528.100}{90.659}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{pharmacological}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{322.999}{528.100}{43.421}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{systems}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{379.601}{528.100}{34.739}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{might}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{427.521}{528.100}{30.843}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{share}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{471.545}{528.100}{49.773}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{common}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{544.660}{408.723}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{neurobiological pathways involving integrated neurobiological circuits that}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{560.986}{408.654}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{when compromised can produce hallucinations. And they hypothesized that}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{577.546}{408.648}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{diverse mechanisms, including dopamine and serotonin activation and}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{593.872}{405.511}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{NMDAR blockage, can disrupt the thalamic gating functions and cortico-}

  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{688.099}{408.702}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{different neurotransmitters and drugs and indirectly through effects on other}

  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{97.550}{408.670}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{Independent of the specific neurotransmitters involved, diverse processes}

  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{175.217}{408.696}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{This common pathway can carry a variety of different features of experience}

  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{208.103}{408.726}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{Ray (2012, 2016) proposes that the diversity in the phenomenology of}

  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{290.668}{408.679}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{cannabinoid receptors) elicits a specific profile of effects that Ray calls a}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{306.994}{408.593}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{mental organ. Each psychedelic drug has effects on a range of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{323.554}{408.698}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{neurotransmitter systems that results in its characteristic effects on neuronal}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{339.880}{408.668}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{activity and on consciousness. His idea is akin to innate modules and modes}

  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{366.101}{356.440}{79.339}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{individualistic}

  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{372.999}{388.665}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{neurotransmitter is a distinct state at least, but a mode of consciousness?}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{400.988}{443.794}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{I would doubt that every neurotransmitter or receptor would each constitute}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{417.547}{443.750}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{separate modes, but they could. But the fact that each transmitter can affect}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{434.107}{443.672}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{different kinds of neurotransmitter systems undermines that possibility of a pure}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{450.666}{161.514}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{serotonin effect, for instance.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{478.655}{443.674}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{I do not think that the individual neurotransmitters are really the best level of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{495.214}{443.632}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{analysis for differences in consciousness. There are major global contrasts with the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{511.541}{443.689}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{waking mode of consciousness that allows us to provide a generalized profile that}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{528.100}{443.681}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{can be induced by diverse mechanisms. It is these generalities that characterize}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{544.660}{443.599}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{ASC—parasympathetic dominance, internal focus of attention, visual experience,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{560.986}{443.680}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{emotional and egoic activation, right hemisphere dominance, etc. that provide}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{577.546}{443.797}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{commonalities for diverse states of the integrative mode of consciousness. If you}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{593.872}{443.839}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{enter into a parasympathetic visionary state, the functional dynamics of that state}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{610.432}{443.714}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{are generally the same whether or not you got there by LSD or ibogaine or ayahuasca}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{626.992}{215.092}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{or some dopaminergic or GABA action.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{134.561}{655.164}{24.676}{ALIUSC7F7F7F}{\ALIUSFontCormorantLight}{46.647}{\ALIUSPullQuoteOpen}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{158.817}{672.568}{322.940}{ALIUSC595959}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{14.694}{I do not think that the individual neurotransmitters}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{158.817}{690.061}{322.963}{ALIUSC595959}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{14.694}{are really the best level of analysis for differences in}

\noindent\begin{tikzpicture}[remember picture,overlay,x=1bp,y=1bp,shift={(current page.north west)}]

  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{46.516}{344.963}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{Michael Winkelman - The evolutionary neuroanthropology of consciousness}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{505.773}{46.516}{14.471}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{79}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{158.817}{81.086}{322.909}{ALIUSC595959}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{14.694}{us to provide a generalized profile that can be}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{364.297}{98.434}{13.761}{ALIUSC7F7F7F}{\ALIUSFontCormorantLight}{46.647}{\ALIUSPullQuoteClose}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{158.817}{98.578}{197.244}{ALIUSC595959}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{14.694}{induced by diverse mechanisms.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{144.197}{443.621}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{So this gets back to how many modes do we need. Perhaps the answer is in terms of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{160.756}{443.697}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{access. Most people can't access the dreaming mode directly from waking}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{177.316}{443.671}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{consciousness and vice versa, although some dream events do produce a rapid}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{193.642}{443.581}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{transition or warp between modes—as well as states. But you can't easily go directly}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{210.202}{443.710}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{from one mode to another unless you have special training, like in lucid dreaming.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{226.761}{443.706}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{So how to apply this to the differentiation of modes and meditative states? How}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{243.088}{443.602}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{many modes do we need to explain meditative experiences? The first criteria ought}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{259.648}{443.689}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{to be access. Can you go willingly from void to bliss? If so, then to me the notion of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{276.207}{443.684}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{different states of consciousness in the integrative mode of consciousness holds. If}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{292.534}{443.631}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{you can't go from one state of consciousness to another and they are functionally}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{309.093}{392.814}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{different in terms of what they can do, then different modes are involved.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{337.315}{443.707}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{I suspect that much of this has been worked out in various Hindu and Buddhist}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{353.641}{443.738}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{traditions. I must confess my original notion of modes of consciousness came from}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{370.201}{443.779}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{some unremembered scholar of meditation. Certainly many Hindi scholars have}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{386.527}{443.684}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{proposed concepts similar to my modes of consciousness in the recognition of deep}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{403.087}{443.594}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{sleep, dream, waking and one or more transcendental forms of consciousness. These}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{419.646}{443.788}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{scholars may well have worked out this question of modes for us on the basis of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{436.206}{162.005}{ALIUSC000000}{\ALIUSFontCormorantMedium}{13.528}{phenomenology and function.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{464.257}{442.959}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{Much of your research conducted in the last two decades was dedicated to the study of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{478.717}{442.904}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{hallucinogens. While it is very common in the field of psychedelic studies to endorse a}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{493.411}{442.859}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{non-naturalistic framework positing that hallucinogenic entities have some kind of}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{507.871}{442.848}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{objective reality (e.g., Luke, 2017; Strassman, Wojtowicz, Luna, \& Frecska, 2008), you}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{522.565}{442.920}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{have played a key role in defending and promoting a naturalistic account of these}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{537.026}{442.915}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{experiences (other accounts following this path notably include: Kent, 2010; Letheby,}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{551.719}{93.587}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{2016, Chapter 4).}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{578.075}{404.080}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{In a recent paper, you have very explicitly advocated this naturalistic approach:}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{604.197}{407.768}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{[…] we need an academic study of entity encounters that offers a thorough}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{618.658}{407.827}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{examination of the similarities in independent reports by identifying the}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{633.351}{407.816}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{recurrent characteristics common to these experiences. (Winkelman, 2018a, p.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{648.045}{13.031}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{6)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{674.167}{407.804}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{[…] if we simply accept the phenomenological experiences of entities as}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{688.628}{407.881}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{transcendent realities, we commit an error of epistemological naivety.}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{112.637}{703.322}{130.157}{ALIUSC1F8135}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{12.128}{(Winkelman, 2018a, p. 5)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{77.652}{783.303}{117.883}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{ALIUS Bulletin n°3 (2019)}
  \ALIUSPlacedTextContent{402.025}{783.303}{116.144}{ALIUSC767171}{\ALIUSFontLatoLight}{10.729}{aliusresearch.org/bulletin}
\end{tikzpicture}
